To address the problem of exploiting a stack-based buffer overflow by overwriting the return address, we can use a **format string attack**. This technique is commonly used in systems where the program is susceptible to format string vulnerabilities, such as when `printf` or similar functions are used without proper input validation.

---

### **Understanding the Stack Layout**

In the context of a function like `vuln()`:

- The function subtracts `0x68` from the stack pointer (`ESP`), creating a stack frame of 104 bytes.
- The buffer used for the format string is located at `EBP - 0x4c`.
- The return address is located at `EBP - 4`.

Thus, the **distance between the buffer and the return address** is:

```
(EBP - 4) - (EBP - 0x4c) = 0x44 = 68 bytes
```

This means that if the format string can write to the buffer, it can overwrite the return address, which is 68 bytes away.

---

### **Using the `%n` Directive**

The `%n` directive in `printf`-family functions is used to write the number of characters printed so far to an integer pointer. For example:

```c
printf("%n", &buffer);
```

This will write the number of characters printed (e.g., 4 if the format string is `"AAAA"`) to the memory address stored in `buffer`.

---

### **Constructing the Format String**

To overwrite the return address, we can use a format string that includes multiple `%n` directives, allowing us to write arbitrary data to the buffer and eventually overwrite the return address.

For example:

char buffer[100];
